  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_949f0976-fee9-415d-931a-04b72b8560c8'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);

<span id="freeTextContainer17174142450326642348">Titus Groan is seven years old. Lord and heir to the crumbling castle Gormenghast. A gothic labyrinth of roofs and turrets, cloisters and corridors, stairwells and dungeons, it is also the cobwebbed kingdom of Byzantine government and age-old rituals, a world primed to implode beneath the weight of centuries of intrigue, treachery, and death. Steerpike, who began his climb</span>
  <span id="freeText17174142450326642348" style="display:none">Titus Groan is seven years old. Lord and heir to the crumbling castle Gormenghast. A gothic labyrinth of roofs and turrets, cloisters and corridors, stairwells and dungeons, it is also the cobwebbed kingdom of Byzantine government and age-old rituals, a world primed to implode beneath the weight of centuries of intrigue, treachery, and death. Steerpike, who began his climb across the roofs when Titus was born, is now ascending the spiral staircase to the heart of the castle, and in his wake lie imprisonment, manipulation, and murder.<br /><br /><i>Gormenghast</i> is the second volume in Mervyn Peake’s widely acclaimed trilogy, but it is much more than a sequel to <i>Titus Groan</i>—it is an enrichment and deepening of that book.<br /><br />The Gormenghast Trilogy ranks as one of the twentieth century’s most remarkable feats of imaginative writing.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17174142450326642348" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/24339931-paul"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1602382069i/24339931._UX100_CR0,38,100,100_.jpg" /></a>

<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/24339931-paul">Paul</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">I'd recommend starting at the beginning. 'Titus Groan' provides a good foundation towards understanding the story's characters and setting, and 'Gorme<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">I'd recommend starting at the beginning. 'Titus Groan' provides a good foundation towards understanding the story's characters and setting, and 'Gormenghast' relies a good bit on that knowledge. I also feel it takes some time to get used to Peakes' weighty style. He is not like our modern writers; He is a poet and an artist. His pages are long and deep and thick with detail. But oh is it worth it! <a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>
</div>

<span id="freeTextContainer1129970262748052929"><br/>I like <i>Titus Groan</i> very much, but I like <i>Gormenghast</i> more. <br/><br/>The visual set pieces are equally vivid, but the style seems less labored, more fluid--less like cubist painting and more like a movie photographed by a cinematographer with a unique and eccentric palette. At first I thought this was principally due to Peake's maturing style--and I still believe that this is an important factor--but I have also come to understand that the growing ease in style, the flow of the narrative, has changed beca</span>
  <span id="freeText1129970262748052929" style="display:none"><br />I like <i>Titus Groan</i> very much, but I like <i>Gormenghast</i> more. <br /><br />The visual set pieces are equally vivid, but the style seems less labored, more fluid--less like cubist painting and more like a movie photographed by a cinematographer with a unique and eccentric palette. At first I thought this was principally due to Peake's maturing style--and I still believe that this is an important factor--but I have also come to understand that the growing ease in style, the flow of the narrative, has changed because Gormenghast, and Titus too, have changed. <br /><br />This place and this prince, both circumscribed and determined by tradition, now live in an unstable world undermined by crime and flood. The earl has entered into the full freedom of young manhood, conscious of the confines of tradition but no longer bound by its restrictions.<br /><br />I look forward with delight to the last volume of the series.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1129970262748052929" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_586828479" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/586828479" />
    <a title="Vit Babenco" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/19283284-vit-babenco"><img alt="Vit Babenco" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1365604577p2/19283284.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/586828479?book_show_action=true">Apr 10, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Vit Babenco" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Vit Babenco" href="/user/show/19283284-vit-babenco">Vit Babenco</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer586828479" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer617974022969063075"><i>Gormenghast</i> is a tale of mysteries… <i>Gormenghast</i> is a story of magic…<br/><i>Gormenghast</i> is written with the mysterious and magical language…<br/><blockquote>Titus is seven. His confines, Gormenghast. Suckled on shadows; weaned, as it were, on webs of ritual: for his ears, echoes, for his eyes, a labyrinth of stone: and yet within his body something other – other than this umbrageous legacy. For first and ever foremost he is <i>child</i>.<br/>A ritual, more compelling than ever man devised, is fighting anchored darkness. A ritual of</blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText617974022969063075" style="display:none"><i>Gormenghast</i> is a tale of mysteries… <i>Gormenghast</i> is a story of magic…<br /><i>Gormenghast</i> is written with the mysterious and magical language…<br /><blockquote>Titus is seven. His confines, Gormenghast. Suckled on shadows; weaned, as it were, on webs of ritual: for his ears, echoes, for his eyes, a labyrinth of stone: and yet within his body something other – other than this umbrageous legacy. For first and ever foremost he is <i>child</i>.<br />A ritual, more compelling than ever man devised, is fighting anchored darkness. A ritual of the blood; of the jumping blood. These quicks of sentience owe nothing to his forbears, but to those feckless hosts, a trillion deep, of the globe’s childhood.</blockquote><br /><i>Gormenghast</i> is a novel of growing up… <i>Gormenghast</i> is a parable of rebellion – if the putrid traditions turn into a garrote and become the personification of evil, they must be demolished…<br /><blockquote>He raised his head and gazed across the thousands of faces below him. He nodded his head in a kind of pompous approval as yet another carving was tossed into the great bonfire. He counted a score of towers to his left. ‘All mine…’ he said, but the words sounded emptily in his head when suddenly something happened which blew his terror and his hope sky-high, which filled him with a joy too huge for him to contain, which took him and shook him out of his indecisions, and swept him into a land of hectic and cruel brilliance, of black glades, and of a magic insupportable.</blockquote><br />Good is face to face with evil… Good must win… Otherwise, the old black decay will strangle the new green sprouts.</span>
  <a data-text-id="617974022969063075" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_23324007" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/23324007" />
    <a title="Cecily" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1199525-cecily"><img alt="Cecily" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1480285828p2/1199525.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/23324007?book_show_action=true">May 30, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cecily" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cecily" href="/user/show/1199525-cecily">Cecily</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781585679454" href="/book/show/3213508-gormenghast">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1199525-cecily?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1199525-cecily?shelf=magical-realism">magical-realism</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1199525-cecily?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1199525-cecily?shelf=gormenghast-peake">gormenghast-peake</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer23324007" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10870418514534985318">The sequel to the wonderful Titus Groan (which I reviewed <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/23324003' rel='nofollow noopener'>HERE</a>). At his christening, Titus, heir to the earldom of Gormenghast (accidentally) ripped the ancient book of ritual and at his earling (aged 2) he blasphemed again by removing sacred objects and casting them into the lake. That congenital rebellion comes to fruition in this book.<br/> <br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1491863674i/22455790._SY540_.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><i>Peake's illustration of Irma Prunesquallor</i><br/><br/><b>The Dead</b><br/><br/>It starts by summarising the ghostly demise of key characters from the first book and the mark they have left</span>
  <span id="freeText10870418514534985318" style="display:none">The sequel to the wonderful Titus Groan (which I reviewed <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/23324003" rel="nofollow noopener">HERE</a>). At his christening, Titus, heir to the earldom of Gormenghast (accidentally) ripped the ancient book of ritual and at his earling (aged 2) he blasphemed again by removing sacred objects and casting them into the lake. That congenital rebellion comes to fruition in this book.<br> <br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1491863674i/22455790._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><i>Peake's illustration of Irma Prunesquallor</i><br><br><b>The Dead</b><br><br>It starts by summarising the ghostly demise of key characters from the first book and the mark they have left on Titus. Then it does a similar update of key characters who are still alive, hinting at what is to come. This unusual approach sets the tone for a story that is outwardly about a child growing up in the stiflingly ritualistic world of Gormenghast alongside Steerpike’s plotting to gain power, but is really about death and destruction. “There are always eyes” and “days when the living have no substance and the dead are active” leaving a “deathless repercussion”.<br><br><b>Humour</b><br><br>Nevertheless, there is still humour, principally provided by naughty schoolboys (“the boys changed ammunition to paper pellets only after the THIRD death and “a deal of confusion in the hiding of the bodies”!) and eccentric school masters (one “had once made a point of being at least one mental hour ahead of his class... but who had long since decided to pursue knowledge on an equal footing” and another “was pure symbol... even the ingenious system of delegation whereon his greatness rested was itself worked out by another”). Irma Prunesquallor also provides plenty of comic mileage (e.g. creating a fake bosom with a hot water bottle and striving to be stylish). As before there are also touches of mystical unreality.<br><br><b>Doom?</b><br><br>Titus grows up, “suckled on shadows, weaned as it were on webs of ritual”, increasingly restless and rebellious, unloved and longing to break free from the shackles of ceremonies “the significance of which had long been lost to the records”. He runs away to the woods a few times, and each time has an experience that changes him and sets him further adrift from his apparent destiny, especially via his growing obsession with Keda’s nameless feral child, referred to as “The Thing”. In fact, the short story, Boy in Darkness (which I reviewed <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/446044244" rel="nofollow noopener">HERE</a>), is an account of one such episode, though it is more allegorical than the other Gormenghast stories.<br><br>Meanwhile, there is a growing sense of evil in the background, with more deaths (some of them grisly) and ultimately destruction on a Biblical scale.<br><br>Overall, this story is very similar to the volume that precedes it, in setting, structure, tone and the extraordinary vividness of the descriptions (“porous shadow-land... not so much a darkness... as something starved for moonbeams.”). The final volume, Titus Alone (which I reviewed <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/23324032" rel="nofollow noopener">HERE</a>) is very different.<br><br>“<i>There is nowhere else... everything comes to Gormenghast.</i>”<br><br>Alternatively, you can have fun looking for parallels with Hamlet: a castle as a prison, murder, madness, revenge, and an alienated heir.<br><br><b>Quotes</b><br><br>•	“porous shadow-land... not so much a darkness... as something starved for moonbeams.”<br>•	“There is nowhere else... you will only tread a circle... everything comes to Gormenghast.”<br>•	“suckled on shadows, weaned as it were on webs of ritual”<br>•	“He was pure symbol... even the ingenious system of delegation whereon his greatness rested was itself worked out by another”<br>•	He “had once made a point of being at least one mental hour ahead of his class... but who had long since decided to pursue knowledge on an equal footing”.<br>•	“a smile she was concocting, a smile more ambitious than she had so far dared to invent. Every muscle in her face was pulling its weight. Not all of them knew in which direction to pull, but their common enthusiasm was formidable.” <br>•	words that are “proud with surrender”. <br>•	“Their presence and the presence of their few belongings... seemed to reinforce the vacancy of their solitude.”<br>•	“A window let in the light and, sometimes, the sun itself, whose beams made of this silent, forgotten landing a cosmos, a firmament of moving motes, brilliantly illumined, an astral and at the same time solar province. Where the sunbeams struck, the floor would flower like a rose, a wall break out in crocus-light, and the banisters would flame like rings of coloured snakes.”<br>•	“the very lack of ghosts... was in itself unnerving”<br>•	It’s positively Wodehousian in places, “made one wonder how this man [Fluke] could share the self-same world with hyacinths and damsels” and his [Perch Prism’s] “eyes with enough rings around them to lasso and strangle at birth any idea that he was under 50”.<br>•	Around the lake “trees arose with a peculiar authority” an one spinney was “in an irritable state”, another “in a condition of suspended excitement” while other trees were variously aloof, mournful, gesticulating, exultant and asleep.<br>•	The boys changed ammunition to paper pellets only after the THIRD death and “a deal of confusion in the hiding of the bodies”!<br>•	“A cloud of starlings moved like a migraine across the upper air”<br>•	“A symbol of something the significance of which had long been lost to the records”<br>•	“Countless candles dribbled with hot wax, and their flames, like little flags, fluttered in the uncharted currents of air.”<br>•	The wick of an enormous oil lamp was “as wide as a sheep’s tongue”!<br>•	“the long drawn hiss of reptilian rain”<br>•	In the snow, “the terrain bulged with the submerged features of a landscape half-remembered”<br>•	“as empty as tongueless bells”<br>•	“as a withered spinster might kiss a spaniel’s nose”<br><br><b>All My Peake Reviews</b><br><br>All my Peake/Gormenghast reviews (including biographies/memoirs and books about his art) are on a shelf, <br><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/list/1199525?shelf=gormenghast-peake&amp;utm_campaign=mybooksnav&amp;utm_content=mybooks_cta&amp;utm_medium=web&amp;utm_source=homepage" rel="nofollow noopener">HERE</a>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10870418514534985318" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_57630899" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/57630899" />
    <a title="J.G. Keely" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/84023-j-g-keely"><img alt="J.G. Keely" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1274333636p2/84023.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/57630899?book_show_action=true">May 28, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="J.G. Keely" class="user" itemprop="url" name="J.G. Keely" href="/user/show/84023-j-g-keely">J.G. Keely</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9782859406127" href="/book/show/39059.Gormenghast">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/84023-j-g-keely?shelf=novel">novel</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/84023-j-g-keely?shelf=uk-and-ireland">uk-and-ireland</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/84023-j-g-keely?shelf=humor">humor</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/84023-j-g-keely?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/84023-j-g-keely?shelf=urban-fantasy">urban-fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/84023-j-g-keely?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/84023-j-g-keely?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer57630899" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16214593884046372786">The Gormenghast books are considered to be the beginning of the 'mannerpunk' genre, and along with Tolkien, Moorecock, and Howard, Peake is one of the fathers of the modern Fantasy genre. Mannerpunk is a genre typified by complex psychology, plots driven by character interaction, and a strong sense of mood. <br/><br/>It is also notable for the characters rather than the world being fantastical. In this sense, mannerpunk, and certainly the Gormenghast books, work in the vein of surrealism (meaning not 'unr</span>
  <span id="freeText16214593884046372786" style="display:none">The Gormenghast books are considered to be the beginning of the 'mannerpunk' genre, and along with Tolkien, Moorecock, and Howard, Peake is one of the fathers of the modern Fantasy genre. Mannerpunk is a genre typified by complex psychology, plots driven by character interaction, and a strong sense of mood. <br /><br />It is also notable for the characters rather than the world being fantastical. In this sense, mannerpunk, and certainly the Gormenghast books, work in the vein of surrealism (meaning not 'unreal', but 'more than the real'); not unlike the Russian Gogol. The genre is based upon the works of authors like Jane Austen, the Brontes, Baroness Orczy, Swift, de Cervantes, and Dumas pere.<br /><br />Peake himself was a polymath, excelling not only as an author, but a poet and artist. He is the only self-portrait in Britain's National Portrait Gallery. As a poet, he has a mastery of language and conceit that places him above popular 'jingle man' Poe.<br /><br />This makes him quite unlike Tolkien, whose long stretches of verse tend to be stilted and unfeeling. Then again, Peake is more passionate than than Tory Tolkien.<br /><br />Despite his mastery of language and evocative characterization, Peake is not an easy read. Indeed, his thick prose and slow pace can quickly tire the mind. Like a skillful chess opponent, Peake demands much of his reader. He is not content to let the reader be a passive escapist, so his work engages and challenges. It would take a great and knowledgeable mind to meet each of these challenges on equal footing, but even we lesser minds may find amusement, shock, and beauty.<br /><br />Peake's original idea was to chronicle the life of a character from birth to death. The first book deals with infancy. The second takes him into adulthood in a sort of bildungsroman. The third involves the adventures of young adulthood. Unfortunately, Peake's slide into dementia prevented any furthering of this vast and witty trove.<br /><br />In literature, Peake may have come the closest to completing a book which balances complex psychology, deep character, poetic style, exploration of reality, and a surreal mixture reality and fancy. Peake's books were very audacious, and though he sometimes fails to reach his own lofty ideals, the really remarkable thing is that sometimes, he doesn't.<br /><br /><a href="http://starsbeetlesandfools.blogspot.com/2012/06/suggested-readings-in-fantasy.html" rel="nofollow noopener">My Fantasy Book Suggestions</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="16214593884046372786" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_663928879" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/663928879" />
    <a title="Bradley" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4213258-bradley"><img alt="Bradley" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1508811508p2/4213258.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/663928879?book_show_action=true">Nov 07, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bradley" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bradley" href="/user/show/4213258-bradley">Bradley</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=2016-shelf">2016-shelf</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer663928879" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14613531139907742984">This classic fantasy still feels almost like allegory and real history wrapped around some of the most beautiful prose in literature. <br/><br/>Seriously. The prose is really fantastic. The names of things are both evocative and as predictive as shadows upon the wall: outlines and no substance.<br/><br/>The same is not true for the characters or the story itself. Titus has many mini-adventures from his childhood through his young adulthood, culminating in his ever-present desire to free himself of his home's odd tr</span>
  <span id="freeText14613531139907742984" style="display:none">This classic fantasy still feels almost like allegory and real history wrapped around some of the most beautiful prose in literature. <br /><br />Seriously. The prose is really fantastic. The names of things are both evocative and as predictive as shadows upon the wall: outlines and no substance.<br /><br />The same is not true for the characters or the story itself. Titus has many mini-adventures from his childhood through his young adulthood, culminating in his ever-present desire to free himself of his home's odd traditions, the duties that will befall him, or even just the shadow of the antagonist that caused so much ruin in the first book. Titus grows up, and this novel is not just a simplified coming of age story. It's as complicated and real life, as full and ripe as all the greatest stories ever told, and it ends with great and satisfying heroism that is turned sour mainly because it only entrenches Titus in the very things he'd spent all his life trying to escape.<br /><br />I feel for him. I really do.<br /><br />There's so much tragedy in Gormenghast, and yet the whole land and the castle feels like a character unto itself, gloriously drawn and full of personality.<br /><br />Anyone could read this without knowing anything about fantasy at all. It kinda transcends genres, turning into something closer to magical realism in traditional fiction despite the fact that it came out long before the term was even coined.<br /><br />Truly, it isn't a book that should be missed if you're a fan of good literature. :) </span>
  <a data-text-id="14613531139907742984" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_6897243" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/6897243" />
    <a title="Paul Bryant" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/416390-paul-bryant"><img alt="Paul Bryant" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1224113172p2/416390.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/6897243?book_show_action=true">Sep 27, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paul Bryant" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paul Bryant" href="/user/show/416390-paul-bryant">Paul Bryant</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/416390-paul-bryant?shelf=novels">novels</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer6897243" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1679387156740827958">(Vaguely spoilerish remarks follow). <br/><br/>Stripped to the bone, Titus Groan and Gormenghast tell a simple story of pre-socialist revolution and why it will inevitably fail. Steerpike, the ostensible villain, the agent of historical transition, is the working class boy from the kitchens who fails to achieve full political consciousness, seeks no solidarity from his co-workers, and decides to infiltrate the system from within, working alone. The toadying middle-classes (Prunesquallor and his sister, al</span>
  <span id="freeText1679387156740827958" style="display:none">(Vaguely spoilerish remarks follow). <br><br>Stripped to the bone, Titus Groan and Gormenghast tell a simple story of pre-socialist revolution and why it will inevitably fail. Steerpike, the ostensible villain, the agent of historical transition, is the working class boy from the kitchens who fails to achieve full political consciousness, seeks no solidarity from his co-workers, and decides to infiltrate the system from within, working alone. The toadying middle-classes (Prunesquallor and his sister, all the teachers) fall to his machinations like ripe fruit. The corrupt upper classes (viciously portrayed as insane or solipsistic and quite indifferent to the suffering of their minions)are equally prostrate before his Machiavellian advances. It appears he will succeed but in order to do so, he must bring the whole grisly Gormenghastian edifice crashing down. We know he must die in the attempt because even to succeed would be a tragedy - he would have no idea what to do with Gormenghast even if he wrested power from the Groans. No Robespierre is he. Would he terminate the intolerable meaningless eternal rituals everyone in the castle is swathed in? Probably not. He would replace the Countess' cats with lizards, or some such boyish whimsy, and fret vacantly, unsure what else to do. His revolution has no point, it has no ideal. Steerpike must die.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380302500i/687639._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>
  <a data-text-id="1679387156740827958" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2970912420" />
    <a title="Paul Sánchez Keighley" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/25180638-paul-s-nchez-keighley"><img alt="Paul Sánchez Keighley" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1557221483p2/25180638.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2970912420?book_show_action=true">Sep 09, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paul Sánchez Keighley" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paul Sánchez Keighley" href="/user/show/25180638-paul-s-nchez-keighley">Paul Sánchez Keighley</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781585679454" href="/book/show/3213508-gormenghast">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25180638-paul-s-nchez-keighley?shelf=gothic">gothic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25180638-paul-s-nchez-keighley?shelf=bildungsroman">bildungsroman</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25180638-paul-s-nchez-keighley?shelf=all-time-favourites">all-time-favourites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25180638-paul-s-nchez-keighley?shelf=best-of-2019">best-of-2019</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25180638-paul-s-nchez-keighley?shelf=c-united-kingdom">c-united-kingdom</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25180638-paul-s-nchez-keighley?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2970912420" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8717691653441022188">Well, I got myself in a good pickle rating <i>
  <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2591638463?book_show_action=false&from_review_page=1' rel='nofollow noopener'>Titus Groan</a>
</i> 5 stars because this book is by every measurable and conceivable standard better than the first. This gets all the stars.<br/><br/><i>Gormenghast</i> is a book you have to Set Time Aside For, and I'm genuinely afraid this will scare off modern readers, what with our diminishing attention spans and insatiable lust for instant gratification. It’s not a book you can pick up while on the intercity bus because you’d end up spending three consecutive bus rides</span>
  <span id="freeText8717691653441022188" style="display:none">Well, I got myself in a good pickle rating <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2591638463?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">Titus Groan</a>
</i> 5 stars because this book is by every measurable and conceivable standard better than the first. This gets all the stars.<br /><br /><i>Gormenghast</i> is a book you have to Set Time Aside For, and I'm genuinely afraid this will scare off modern readers, what with our diminishing attention spans and insatiable lust for instant gratification. It’s not a book you can pick up while on the intercity bus because you’d end up spending three consecutive bus rides reading the description of the way the sun slants onto some flagstones at a certain time of the day.<br /><br />Having said that, Peake has seriously improved as a storyteller. Not as a writer because, blimey, this guy can write, and <i>Titus Groan</i> couldn’t have been better written had the Almighty himself guided the author’s hand. I say he’s improved as a storyteller because in <i>Gormenghast</i> he’s learnt how to quicken the pace, raise the stakes and create a genuinely moving human drama. That is not to say this book isn’t slow; it’s the difference between watching continental drift and watching a glacier calving.<br /><br />So many things threaten to stay in my memory I should make them pay rent: Opus Fluke’s silent laughter, Irma Prunesquallor’s soirée, the surprise for Titus’ 10th birthday, the endless but hypnotising description of Titus’ daydreams evoked by staring at the colours of a marble… This book manages to be magical without containing an ounce of magic and to feel familiar in the most alienating of settings, a moving reminder of the power of literature to spirit us away to worlds previously unimaginable. It is a true wonder that belongs in the highest echelons of modern British literature and I will treasure it like an heirloom.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8717691653441022188" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_593688016" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/593688016" />
    <a title="midnightfaerie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6610433-midnightfaerie"><img alt="midnightfaerie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1318372863p2/6610433.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/593688016?book_show_action=true">Apr 18, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="midnightfaerie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="midnightfaerie" href="/user/show/6610433-midnightfaerie">midnightfaerie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6610433-midnightfaerie?shelf=classics">classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer593688016" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16190684269958409859">An excellent second book in a horrifically creepy trilogy. As the second book in the trilogy, Gormenghast doesn't disappoint with even more eccentric characters and mounting tension with our evil villain, Steerpike. Gormenghast feels as if it's still a part of the first book, it flows so well. In fact, by the way it ended, I almost could have seen the story ending there, and so I'm somewhat perplexed as to how the third book is going to go. For me, the main character out of the myriad of charact</span>
  <span id="freeText16190684269958409859" style="display:none">An excellent second book in a horrifically creepy trilogy. As the second book in the trilogy, Gormenghast doesn't disappoint with even more eccentric characters and mounting tension with our evil villain, Steerpike. Gormenghast feels as if it's still a part of the first book, it flows so well. In fact, by the way it ended, I almost could have seen the story ending there, and so I'm somewhat perplexed as to how the third book is going to go. For me, the main character out of the myriad of characters to choose from, is not, in fact, a person, but the castle Gormenghast itself. So to see our young Titus Groan heading out at the end of the book, makes me wonder if we'll still have flashbacks of the happenings in our beloved keep. I truly hope so. <br /><br />For those of you who haven't read Peake's trilogy, first, I highly encourage you to do so. And second, telling you how this second book ends in no way depletes from this awesome and ominous story. In this edition we see a host of new characters that add some comic relief, a weird sort of romance that doesn't necessarily sets your heart strings plucking, and the loss of some favorite characters. But that's all in a day's events here at Gormenghast. The romance in particular will make you smile, at least at first, and then those of you who, like me, who have been married for awhile, might see the wry irony that comes from years of having to get along with the same person. In fact, it's almost super-human-like to see how fast the couple goes from gooey-eyed love to bickering old couple. But that's only a smart part of the events in this book. One of my favorite parts is the flood. <br /><br />When I was a kid, I loved to swim and often wondered what it would be like to have each room in the house filled with water so that I could swim from one room to another. In essence, this is what happens in the castle. While the adults deal with the deaths, the sickness, and salvaging what items they can, the kids are busy diving from the turrets into the glassy ponds that used to be rooms and courtyards. Of course, I'm completed conflicted in this revelry. Part of me thinks how much fun it would be to be a kid again and be jumping into a lake from my bedroom window, and then the other half of me is horrified and can't help but think, those are stone walls under that water! What's to keep them from smashing their heads in on those walls when they dive? Either way, the imagery is explicit and entertaining and I never tire of reading Peake's style of writing. <br /><br />Probably there are many themes brought up in other reviews: religion or lack thereof, tradition, ritual, etc. And yes, they're there, however, for my being a Peake virgin, I would like to just continue to bask in the loveliness of the dissertation. The prose that seems to flow so effortlessly from Peake's pen is beguiling and enchanting, and I just want to take a bath in these words. I want to submerge myself in the lush detail of this literature and think of Fuchsia-esque like ways to commit suicide for I am so not worthy to even breathe on the book cover of this talented writer. And yes, this is over-the-top dramatic, but I like to think I've been Fuchsia-inspired and I will leave these cheesy sentences as is. <br /><br />Without a doubt, this is a work of classical literature. The form, the style of writing, its uniqueness, the underlying themes of the human condition and its undefinable genre make it so. But more than anything, its huge following and the magical world that Peake creates shows us his genius. I hear the last book is very different from the first two, and I pray it isn't so. <br /><br /><br /><a href="http://classicsdefined.com/gormenghast.html" rel="nofollow noopener"> ClassicsDefined.com</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="16190684269958409859" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_478167317" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/478167317" />
    <a title="Kyle" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11665678-kyle"><img alt="Kyle" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1362814696p2/11665678.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/478167317?book_show_action=true">Dec 15, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kyle" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kyle" href="/user/show/11665678-kyle">Kyle</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781585679454" href="/book/show/3213508-gormenghast">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11665678-kyle?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer478167317" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer732247564317656020">With Titus Groan, Peake awakened me to what is possible when writing pen and brilliant mind are in perfect harmony. He created a tapestry of humanity and community uniquely compendious, woven together with threads of absolutely breathtaking writing. Yet for all its magnificence, it's purpose was still largely to set the foundation for the second book, Gormenghast.<br/><br/>And such a second book it is. Gormenghast is Peake unleashed. In its pages he manages to pry humanity open, examine and play with all </span>
  <span id="freeText732247564317656020" style="display:none">With Titus Groan, Peake awakened me to what is possible when writing pen and brilliant mind are in perfect harmony. He created a tapestry of humanity and community uniquely compendious, woven together with threads of absolutely breathtaking writing. Yet for all its magnificence, it's purpose was still largely to set the foundation for the second book, Gormenghast.<br /><br />And such a second book it is. Gormenghast is Peake unleashed. In its pages he manages to pry humanity open, examine and play with all its viscera, and stitch everything back together in a way of his own choosing. Peake forces the reader to become his toy, his plaything. Like a cat he plays with us, and trapped within the strings of his pages we cannot help put be tugged and batted along. <br /><br />Gormenghast, in its dark and dreamlike way, will demand a heavy mental and emotional price, yet it will reward readers who allow the heavy castle stones and it's inchoate characters to permeate their mind. <br /><br /><i>
  <b>He no longer wanted to kill his foe in darkness and in silence. His lust was to stand naked upon the moonlight stage, with his arms stretched high, and fingers spread, and with the warm fresh blood that soaked them sliding down his wrists, spiralling his arms and steaming in the cold night air - to suddenly drop his hands like talons to his breast and tear it open to expose a heart like a black vegetable- and then, upon the crest of self-exposure, and the sweet glory of wickedness, to create some gesture of supreme defiance, lewd and rare; and then, with the towers of Gormenghast about him, cheat the castle of its jealous right and die of his own evil in the moonbeams.</b>
</i><br /><br />There is more "action" in this, the second book, than was in Titus Groan, and the book itself feels like a faster read. Yet, I found myself taking longer to read it only for the reason that I wanted to savor it. So complete was my enjoyment, so deep was my investment of this book, that I find myself truly mourning Mervyn Peake's death. I bemoan his loss to the world, and I weep whenever I remember that the story of Titus Groan will never be completed. <br /><br />I eagerly await the chance to dive into Titus Alone, the third book, yet I will need to take a small break before I do. I will need to reflect and chew on everything I've already experienced, and prepare myself for the mysterious journey ahead still calling to me from the pages Peake's masterpiece. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="732247564317656020" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Megan Baxter" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5742086-megan-baxter"><img alt="Megan Baxter" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1310153693p2/5742086.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Megan Baxter" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Megan Baxter" href="/user/show/5742086-megan-baxter">Megan Baxter</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">

<div id="review_1911551990" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1911551990" />
    <a title="Stevie Kincade" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/56187822-stevie-kincade"><img alt="Stevie Kincade" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1476505168p2/56187822.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1911551990?book_show_action=true">Feb 21, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stevie Kincade" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stevie Kincade" href="/user/show/56187822-stevie-kincade">Stevie Kincade</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/56187822-stevie-kincade?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/56187822-stevie-kincade?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/56187822-stevie-kincade?shelf=audiobooks">audiobooks</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1911551990" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13795402528348044659"><i>A kind of lull had settled upon the castle. It was not that events were lacking, but that even those of major importance had about them a sense of unreality. It was as though some strange wheel of destiny had brought the earth its pre-ordained lacuna</i><br/><br/>I reviewed <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1738409100' rel='nofollow noopener'>Titus Groan</a> earlier today and my main bone of contention is that "Gormenghast", the 2nd book should really be called "Titus Groan" and the first book should be called "Gormenghast". Titus is only an infant in "Titus Groan" but in "Gormengh</span>
  <span id="freeText13795402528348044659" style="display:none"><i>A kind of lull had settled upon the castle. It was not that events were lacking, but that even those of major importance had about them a sense of unreality. It was as though some strange wheel of destiny had brought the earth its pre-ordained lacuna</i><br /><br />I reviewed <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1738409100" rel="nofollow noopener">Titus Groan</a> earlier today and my main bone of contention is that "Gormenghast", the 2nd book should really be called "Titus Groan" and the first book should be called "Gormenghast". Titus is only an infant in "Titus Groan" but in "Gormenghast" he comes to the fore as our protagonist, while the first book is largely about the goings-on in the castle <i>Gormenghast</i>.<br /><br />I could more or less cut and paste that review here: Stunning, constantly surprising prose, farcical humour, plenty of atmosphere, super strange tone and a bare skerrick of plot.<br /><br />It is a testament to Peake's writing that in the first half of the book so little happens that the main plot point is "Urma Prunesquallor plans a party" and I still loved every minute of it.<br /><br />In "Titus Groan" we spend a lot of time with Steerpike the up-jumped kitchen hand and he morphs from ambitious and self centred in the first book into a moustache twirling villain. We get plenty of call backs to the first novel, a delightfully farcical romance and a coming of age story. More happens in the last 1/5th of this novel than in the first two combined. Did I mention that prose? <br /><br /><i>He had emptied the bright goblet of romance; at a single gulp he had emptied it. The glass of it lay scattered on the floor. </i><br /><br />The humour turns decidedly blacker in "Gormenghast" as more than one beloved character gets dispatched without a hint of drama or pathos. Perhaps another thing Peake originated could be the "unsentimental ironic death scene". I loved Bellgrove's rise to professor, Urma's party and subsequent romance and the names of the professors: Perch-Prism, Flanellcat, Shred, Shrivell and Deadyawn.<br /> <br />I will continue to sing the praises of narrator Robert Whitfield (Simon Vance) and insist that you listen to no other audio version than this one. Track it down! His voicing of Urma Prunesquallor is beyond hilarious and she becomes one of the major characters in this novel. RW's narration was a major factor in how much I enjoyed listening to this story that can admittedly be a bit slow in parts.<br /><br />If you want to read something that is both dark and humorous, uplifting and disconcerting and quite amazingly written nearly 70 years ago, give Peake a try. <br /><br /><i> Life is too fleet for onomatopoeia</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="13795402528348044659" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer3280504021623001923">I know it's a classic, I know it's groundbreaking and a phenomenal creative achievement. I know his vision was superb, his plotting exact, his characters supremely well-observed - sympathetic and horrifying and humorous in equal measures, making the storyline more complex than a simple tale of betrayal and vengeance (inhale).   <span id="freeText3280504021623001923" style="display:none">I know it's a classic, I know it's groundbreaking and a phenomenal creative achievement. I know his vision was superb, his plotting exact, his characters supremely well-observed - sympathetic and horrifying and humorous in equal measures, making the storyline more complex than a simple tale of betrayal and vengeance (inhale). I know his prose is spectacular...<br /><br />...but bloody hellfire does there have to be so MUCH of it?<br /><br />Dear Gods. I did get to the end this time (it's previously defeated me on a couple of occasions) but talk about long-winded. The ending is superb, but wading though the water and the weather and the masonry, through the pointless (though perfectly observed) plotlines, through the endless filigree details of characters' emotional motivations, really took an effort.<br /><br />I know I'm doing it a disservice with three stars - really wish I'd enjoyed it more.</span>

<div id="review_1688458938" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1688458938" />
    <a title="Rory" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/43447785-rory"><img alt="Rory" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1467814331p2/43447785.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1688458938?book_show_action=true">Jul 05, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rory" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rory" href="/user/show/43447785-rory">Rory</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43447785-rory?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43447785-rory?shelf=in-omnibus">in-omnibus</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1688458938" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17334090827463730782">10/10<br/><br/>Titus Groan was one of the very best books I've ever read. It had a fantastic setting, captivating characters, and, most importantly, outstanding prose. It's sequel, Gormenghast, has all of these, and one more thing as well: a plot. <br/>Whereas the plot of Titus Groan seemed to take a backseat to the other aspects of the book (not that I minded, I was lost in Peake's wonderful writing), the story in Gormenghast is driven forwards by Steerpike's continued machinations and becomes exciting to rea</span>
  <span id="freeText17334090827463730782" style="display:none">10/10<br /><br />Titus Groan was one of the very best books I've ever read. It had a fantastic setting, captivating characters, and, most importantly, outstanding prose. It's sequel, Gormenghast, has all of these, and one more thing as well: a plot. <br />Whereas the plot of Titus Groan seemed to take a backseat to the other aspects of the book (not that I minded, I was lost in Peake's wonderful writing), the story in Gormenghast is driven forwards by Steerpike's continued machinations and becomes exciting to read, as well as just beautiful. On top of this, there was also some decent character development, which Titus Groan also lacked (although, now I think on it, that might have been deliberate, a way for Peake to show the unchanging nature of Gormenghast). In my opinion, it was pretty much perfection.<br />I have only one, minor, complaint about this book, which is that it didn't seem quite as vivid as Titus Groan. People talk about Peake painting a picture with words, and the pictures from the first book stick in my head more than those from the second. I suspect, however, that this is a byproduct of the faster plot; I spent less time savouring each sentence because I was more eager to read the next one.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17334090827463730782" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">
          

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review1688458938">

<div id="review_662698053" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/662698053" />
    <a title="Metaphorosis" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7706283-metaphorosis"><img alt="Metaphorosis" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1399210069p2/7706283.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/662698053?book_show_action=true">Jul 09, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Metaphorosis" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Metaphorosis" href="/user/show/7706283-metaphorosis">Metaphorosis</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781585679454" href="/book/show/3213508-gormenghast">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7706283-metaphorosis?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7706283-metaphorosis?shelf=2013-rev">2013-rev</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer662698053" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer169000556670977159">I think I'd only read <i>Gormenghast</i> (the book) once before, maybe twice, whereas I've read the first book, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/39063.Titus_Groan__Gormenghast___1_' title='Titus Groan (Gormenghast, #1) by Mervyn Peake' rel='nofollow noopener'>Titus Groan</a>, multiple times. Unsurprisingly, I didn't remember this second book nearly as well as I did the first.<br/><br/>The second book is also simply not as strong a book as the first. <i>Titus Groan</i> is chock full of dark images and heavy symbolism seen through an obscuring cloud of gloom. In <i>Gormenghast,</i> in contrast, Peake literally comes straight out and <i>tells</i> the reader what the symbols are. This </span>
  <span id="freeText169000556670977159" style="display:none">I think I'd only read <i>Gormenghast</i> (the book) once before, maybe twice, whereas I've read the first book, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/39063.Titus_Groan__Gormenghast___1_" title="Titus Groan (Gormenghast, #1) by Mervyn Peake" rel="nofollow noopener">Titus Groan</a>, multiple times. Unsurprisingly, I didn't remember this second book nearly as well as I did the first.<br /><br />The second book is also simply not as strong a book as the first. <i>Titus Groan</i> is chock full of dark images and heavy symbolism seen through an obscuring cloud of gloom. In <i>Gormenghast,</i> in contrast, Peake literally comes straight out and <i>tells</i> the reader what the symbols are. This is most evident in the first 50-100 pages, but also occurs toward the end, when Titus is making his plans for the future. One character, <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ the 'Thing',  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> is clearly created purely for symbolic purposes, and it's disconcerting that Peake removes all possibility of mystery by telling us exactly what the character's meaning is.<br /><br />That character is itself an awkward construct. While most of the new characters introduced in this book are decently put together - the Professors as a corps, and to some extent as individuals, some, such as this special one, seem more like afterthoughts. It's incongruous, then, for this one to play (we're told) such a central role on Titus' life.<br /><br />Peake does a nice job of reconnecting with other characters, and tieing them back into the castle's (and Titus') story. The Countess, the Bright Carvers, and the Doctor all play important roles. Flay, while central, seems less fully developed, which is odd, since it seems clear that Peake fully intended this return to prominence. Peake also resolves Fuchsia's plotline in a seemingly offhanded manner.<br /><br />The plot itself itself is interesting. Steerpike develops in a credible way. There's a key flood whose mechanics are questionable, but then Gormenghast is not tied too closely to reality. There's quite a lot of humor in the book.<br /><br />There are a number of consistency errors - several related to Fuchsia's age, and one to Titus' location - signs of rushed or limited editing, perhaps. And there are scattered instances of words misused ('flaunt' for 'flout'), which would be less surprising in a writer less careful than Peake.<br /><br />I've focused on the negative, because I found the book disappointing in comparison with its predecessor, but that's not to say it isn't a good read. All in all, it's a sturdy successor to <i>Titus Groan,</i> but lacking the magic of the first volume. Worth reading for people who were caught up in the characters that inhabit the castle, but less so for those who mainly liked the imagery.<br /><br />3.5 stars rounded up to 4.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="169000556670977159" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_492921143" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/492921143" />
    <a title="Arukiyomi" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/161559-arukiyomi"><img alt="Arukiyomi" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1193221954p2/161559.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/492921143?book_show_action=true">Jun 09, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Arukiyomi" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Arukiyomi" href="/user/show/161559-arukiyomi">Arukiyomi</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/161559-arukiyomi?shelf=1001-books">1001-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer492921143" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17553223020950372786"><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1520790177i/25197911.png' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><strong>
  <em>...the best way to approach it all is to lie back and drift in the stream</em>
</strong><br/><br/>As I watched the protagonist ride off into the sunset at the close of the second part of Peake’s vastly underrated trilogy, I couldn’t help but think that he’d mixed up the titles of the first two installments. This novel, not the first, is about Titus Groan whereas the first novel, not this one, is about Gormenghast.<br/><br/>But that is a negligible criticism for two works of writing which have been all but forgotten in the hal</span>
  <span id="freeText17553223020950372786" style="display:none"><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1520790177i/25197911.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><strong>
  <em>...the best way to approach it all is to lie back and drift in the stream</em>
</strong><br><br>As I watched the protagonist ride off into the sunset at the close of the second part of Peake’s vastly underrated trilogy, I couldn’t help but think that he’d mixed up the titles of the first two installments. This novel, not the first, is about Titus Groan whereas the first novel, not this one, is about Gormenghast.<br><br>But that is a negligible criticism for two works of writing which have been all but forgotten in the half century since they were written. A lot of what I wrote for Titus Groan applies yet again, and more so: the prose perfectly matches the world Peake has created; the characters only deepen their Dickensian charm; Steerpike’s scheming reaches its climax; and the best way to approach it all is to lie back and drift in the stream.<br><br>At times, I have to say, I wondered if Peake was a bit too ponderous. Irma Prunesquallor’s love life seems a bit too tangential to hold my attention for as long as was required. But having got through that, and in the latter third of this volume, the pace quickens as political currents clash.<br><br>Titus Groan’s growing pains form an important part of the storyline, hence my thoughts that the novel’s name is misleading. As he grows more aware that he is different from those around him, his desire for freedom creates a tension between his duty and his yearnings. This adds yet another layer of tension to the latter half of the book which keeps the pages turning.<br><br>If you haven’t read any of Peake’s work at all, I do suggest you start at the beginning and read Titus Groan. Starting with Gormenghast would be a mistake as there’s a lot you won’t realise the significance of without having read the first installment.<br><br>I suppose the ultimate test of the first two novels is whether you actually finish the trilogy and read the third book, Titus Alone. I’m in no hurry, but if I do come across a secondhand copy one day, I’ll probably buy it just to finish off the series and at least doff my hat to Mervyn Peake’s memory.<br><br></span>
  <a data-text-id="17553223020950372786" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_185604937" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/185604937" />
    <a title="Antonis" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4749075-antonis"><img alt="Antonis" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1535985971p2/4749075.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/185604937?book_show_action=true">Jul 16, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Antonis" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Antonis" href="/user/show/4749075-antonis">Antonis</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781585679454" href="/book/show/3213508-gormenghast">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer185604937" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer836163177496685661"><b>5 / 5</b><br/><br/>Gormenghast by Mervyn Peake is a unique book. It is an incomparable masterpiece by one of the most amazing and interesting authors I have ever had the chance to read. And I say “amazing” with the true meaning of the word as this is a book that will amaze you constantly while reading it. See, Peake’s writing is not like anything one might have read. I’m can safely say that Peake must have been a very bold and perceptive man. He sees things that are obvious but always stay out of sight, he ex</span>
  <span id="freeText836163177496685661" style="display:none"><b>5 / 5</b><br /><br />Gormenghast by Mervyn Peake is a unique book. It is an incomparable masterpiece by one of the most amazing and interesting authors I have ever had the chance to read. And I say “amazing” with the true meaning of the word as this is a book that will amaze you constantly while reading it. See, Peake’s writing is not like anything one might have read. I’m can safely say that Peake must have been a very bold and perceptive man. He sees things that are obvious but always stay out of sight, he explores thoughts that everybody has but nobody admits or realizes. But I think I’m getting carried away, let’s do this in my usual organized way.<br /><br /><b>Characters:</b><br />To say that Peake’s characters are simply real-like and multidimensional is to underestimate him. To mention that his characters are different, strange and interesting is just scratching the surface. His characters truly live, they are everywhere and nowhere. Everywhere because the reader understands them so well, feels their every thought and sensation in a way. They are so unique and real that it feels as if you’ve known them forever. But they are also nowhere, as they so strange and unique that one can not imagine them existing outside of the encompassing setting of Gormenghast, There can not really be copies anywhere.<br />Even through the third-person perspective, Peake presents them in a very personal level. The reader can feel what they feel, know what they perceive and read most of their thoughts. This very subjective and personal way of coming in contact with each character makes each one of them a protagonist. Nobody is just good or bad, there’s no such thing as black and white in Gormenghast. Each reader loves and hates everybody on various degrees according to their own psyche.<br /><br /><b>Plot:</b><br />To actually try to create a paragraph talking about plot in a Mervyn Peake book feels like an exercise in futility, not to say a downright silly notion. This book is about progression &amp; turmoil but mostly it’s about change; change in all ways and levels imaginable. In this book, everything changes, and I’m not talking just about the characters.<br /><br /><b>Pacing:</b><br />As with Peake’s first book, pacing here is also slow. Actually it’s very slow, sometimes sluggishly slow but in an unhurried and deliberate way. But this is exactly what Peake is. There’s no way that this book would be what it is if it was a fast page-turner. Everything needs time here: the reader must take time to absorb the magic and all the details from Peake’s wonderful writing, events need time to happen, characters need time to change, the whole Gormenghast needs time to adjust and evolve. This is not a book for fast consumption and quick satisfaction. This is a grand and exquisite gourmet that must be savored slowly and carefully lest one miss something great!<br /><br /><b>Writing:</b><br />Let me say that upfront. There’s no writer like Mervyn Peake and you haven’t read anything like it. Peake, while verbose and eloquent, writes with clarity and lucidity, with an obvious purpose and aim, with each word flowing effortlessly into sentences and then into paragraphs that reading this book is like magic. He describes things that are in front of our eyes but we never actually see, he dares dive deep into the human psyche, into feelings we know exist but we avoid facing and brings them up in front of our eyes. Exactly like an experienced photographer who can see things with a different eye and then present that to us in a way we’ve never seen it before. Or like a skilled psychologist who knows which questions to ask to make you realize things about yourself you always knew were there but you never recognized. Well, this is exactly how Peake writes.<br />But enough of my own words, let me give you some quotes! :)<br /><br /> <blockquote>
  <i>To say that the frozen silence contracted itself into a yet higher globe of ice were to under-rate the exquisite tension and to shroud it in words. The atmosphere had become a physical sensation. As when, before a masterpiece, the acid throat contracts, and words are millstones, so when the supernaturally outlandish happens and a masterpiece is launched through the medium of human gesture, then all human volition is withered at the source and the heart of action stops beating.<br /> Such a moment was this. Irma, a stalagmite of crimson stone, knew, for all the riot of her veins that a page had turned over. At chapter forty? O no! At chapter one, for she had never lived before save in a pulseless preface.<br /> How long did they remain thus? How many times had the earth moved round the sun? How many times had the great blue whales of the northern waters risen to spurt their fountains at the sky? How many reed-bucks had fallen to the claws of how many leopards, while that sublime unit of two-figure statuary remained motionless? It is fruitless to ask. The clocks of the world stood still or should have done.</i>
</blockquote><br /> <blockquote>
  <i>Indeed he had worn that piece of furniture - or symbol of bone-laziness - into such a shape as made the descent of any other body than his own into that crater of undulating horsehair a hazardous enterprise</i>
</blockquote><br /> <blockquote>
  <i>Meanwhile Bellgrove had been savouring love's rare aperitif, the ageless language of the eyes.</i>
</blockquote><br /> <blockquote>
  <i>Noon, ripe as thunder and silent as thought, had fled unfingered.</i>
</blockquote><br /> <blockquote>
  <i>He knew that he was caught up in one of those stretches of time when for anything to happen normally would be abnormal. The dawn was too tense and highly charged for any common happening to survive.</i>
</blockquote><br /> <blockquote>
  <i>He had emptied the bright goblet of romance; at a single gulp he had emptied it. The glass of it lay scattered on the floor.</i>
</blockquote><br /> <blockquote>
  <i>His mother stood before him like a monument. He saw her great outline through the blur of his weakness and his passion. She made no movement at all. </i>
</blockquote><br /><br />Ok I will stop now, I have pages of these quotes and they lose their magic when read out of context. But they are so powerful, oh some of them they truly are!<br /><br /><b>Conclusion:</b><br />There’s not much more to say at this point. This is not an easy book to read, and I’m afraid many might be left disappointed. But to those who read this with an open mind and do so properly so that they can really enjoy it for what it is, it will be a exceptional experience. Ultimately, Mervyn Peake’s Gormenghast is a unique book, a wonderful book, a celebration to writing and literature! Read it!<br /><br /><b>5 stars out of 5 !!</b></span>
  <a data-text-id="836163177496685661" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
