<div id="review_346969245" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/346969245" />
    <a title="Leah" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5456299-leah"><img alt="Leah" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1352111252p2/5456299.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/346969245?book_show_action=true">Jun 12, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Leah" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Leah" href="/user/show/5456299-leah">Leah</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781585679454" href="/book/show/3213508-gormenghast">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5456299-leah?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer346969245" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11012472021872639044">An extremely curious experience.<br/><br/>I had no idea what to expect, except that I was expecting a lot. This book is often mentioned in the same breath as that father of modern epic fantasy, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/33.The_Lord_of_the_Rings__Lord_of_the_Rings__1_3_' title='The Lord of the Rings (Lord of the Rings #1-3) by J.R.R. Tolkien' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Lord of the Rings</a>, and spoken of in hushed, reverent tones as a fantasy classic. I've put it on my fantasy shelf for want of a better place, but there is little of what you generally associate with that genre here. In fact, but for the immense size and vast proportions of Gormenghast, this story could be histor</span>
  <span id="freeText11012472021872639044" style="display:none">An extremely curious experience.<br /><br />I had no idea what to expect, except that I was expecting a lot. This book is often mentioned in the same breath as that father of modern epic fantasy, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/33.The_Lord_of_the_Rings__Lord_of_the_Rings__1_3_" title="The Lord of the Rings (Lord of the Rings #1-3) by J.R.R. Tolkien" rel="nofollow noopener">The Lord of the Rings</a>, and spoken of in hushed, reverent tones as a fantasy classic. I've put it on my fantasy shelf for want of a better place, but there is little of what you generally associate with that genre here. In fact, but for the immense size and vast proportions of Gormenghast, this story could be historical fiction. There is no magic, no wizards, no dragons or Dark Lords, no quests, no travelling between worlds or multiple races of beings (you could count the Carvers as another race, I suppose, but I thought of them more as a different nationality, living in a different part of the world). There are just people, living in this mass of stones.<br /><br />And what people there are! Larger than life, with odd names and bizarre exaggerated characteristics, most of the characters in this novel would feel just as comfortable in a Dickens story or in Wonderland as they do in Gormenghast. The story flicks wildly between the drastically serious actions of Steerpike, the somewhat whinging adolescent thoughts of Titus, and the incredible, over-the-top antics of Gormenghast's teaching staff. It is in this last section that most of the Dickensian nonsense takes place, that makes the book feel less like a fantasy than a farce. At times stupidly entertaining, I nevertheless found myself wondering what it all had to do with the story. This was probably the wrong way to approach the book. <br /><br />Apart from the comical teachers, the most memorable and noticeable thing about this book is the style of writing, or more accurately, the level and consistency of description Peake upholds. I have never read a book whose author clearly had such strong concepts of scenery, and skill enough to convey those concepts to the reader. Often it felt like I was having a painting described to me, one moment caught in time, filled with meaning and movement and colour, but ultimately still. Eventually, I felt like the story was truly being told in a series of still scenes, like walking past a series of paintings, cleverly positioned to make me think I was watching a film. It was all a trick of the light.<br /><br />This does mean, however, that the book is a lot longer than the very simple story would have you believe. I did often find myself feeling sluggish, bogged down in description and scene-setting and inner monologues when I just wanted to know what happened next. Additionally, I felt that character motivation was probably not Peake's strong point, especially where Titus and the Thing were concerned. That storyline left me cold. His reasoning could have used some questioning, or some editing, or possibly both.<br /><br />The concept of Gormenghast castle left a deep impression on me. I loved the feeling of size Peake injected into the story, where characters can walk for hours and still be within the castle, where a boy can get lost in a string of tunnels and corridors he never knew existed, where whole wings and buildings and floors haven't been seen, let alone occupied, in centuries. Areas of the castle are named like suburbs, all rooms are furnished even if they have been long forgotten (including a dust-covered table that Flay discovers, laid for two countless years ago, <i>a la</i> Miss Havisham in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2623.Great_Expectations" title="Great Expectations by Charles Dickens" rel="nofollow noopener">Great Expectations</a>). I love this idea. Combined with the sense of decay, of losing the battle against nature, of a shrinking population going about their business as their home disintegrates around their ears, the impression is a powerful one, something that gave the story an intense atmosphere.<br /><br />In the end, this book is unlike anything I've read before, or am likely to read again. A true original, a challenging read, a visual feast and a strange journey, I feel content at having finished this one for now, although I won't discount reading the others if they come my way.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11012472021872639044" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_76515624" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/76515624" />
    <a title="Michael" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1036930-michael"><img alt="Michael" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1583425448p2/1036930.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/76515624?book_show_action=true">Nov 02, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael" href="/user/show/1036930-michael">Michael</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780879514266" href="/book/show/3170466-gormenghast">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1036930-michael?shelf=1950s">1950s</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1036930-michael?shelf=goodest-reads-2009">goodest-reads-2009</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1036930-michael?shelf=fiction-that-speculates">fiction-that-speculates</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer76515624" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7325030476582832937"><br/>(Update: After reading this book last year, I chose to give it 4 stars, my argument being that I was detracting one star for the slow pacing. Then at the beginning of 2010, I determined that Gormenghast was #2 on my "10 Goodest Reads From 2009" list. (China Mieville's The Scar came in #1.) So, umm, I'm retroactively giving this'n five stars, because I was clearly on crack when I didn't give it 5 before. Below is the review of the book, which hasn't been changed.) <br/><br/><br/>At Gormenghast castle--a castle </span>
  <span id="freeText7325030476582832937" style="display:none"><br />(Update: After reading this book last year, I chose to give it 4 stars, my argument being that I was detracting one star for the slow pacing. Then at the beginning of 2010, I determined that Gormenghast was #2 on my "10 Goodest Reads From 2009" list. (China Mieville's The Scar came in #1.) So, umm, I'm retroactively giving this'n five stars, because I was clearly on crack when I didn't give it 5 before. Below is the review of the book, which hasn't been changed.) <br /><br /><br />At Gormenghast castle--a castle so sprawling and gargantuan that huge sections have fallen into disrepair and been forgotten entirely; a castle where the Queen wanders the halls silently with hundreds of white cats following her; a castle where everyone (almost everyone) follows rituals so old that none know their meaning any longer; a castle where teachers sleep through class periods while students invent dangerous games and hide the bodies of any children that die during them--Titus Groan is growing up. He's to be the king of Gormenghast, but he quickly grows to detest the meaningless rituals that infect his every day. <br /><br />As Titus rebels against his station, Steerpike continues his political ascent. And, unlike most villains, Steerpike changes in very unpleasant ways as he continues to sacrifice his scruples for greater power. By the end of this book, the bizarre upstart from the first novel has become quite a frightening figure. <br /><br />The Gormenghast trilogy is something very unique in fantasy. It has all the originality one could want from a fantasy world. And it is truly character-driven. Although the characters feel at first like funhouse mirror distortions of real people (in large part due to their farcical names and often distorted physical features), they begin to feel much more real as the book progresses. <br /><br />Also, the story is amazing. Although it would be impossible to predict what was going to happen early on, everything fits together and seems inevitable when looking back after it's all over. It's epic; it's sad; it's hilarious; it's entirely original, and the writing is often amazing. It's an understatement to say Mervyn Peake has a way with words. <br /><br />One of the faults I see isn't really that much of a fault, and more just a comment: one particular side-plot absorbs much of the first half of the book. In the end, this side-plot doesn't seem necessary. But, that part of the story provides a humorous diversion from the gloomy main storyline, where bodies are piling up and characters are not so very happy. My one complaint is the pace of the book. This book is, like Titus Groan, quite slow. And, again, I noticed the slowness at the time I should most NOT be noticing it: when suspense was building for the climax. The climax took forever. <br /><br />This book is very close to five stars. But, since it suffers from the same fault as the first novel, I'm witholding one star. And I'm curious to see what happens in the final volume of the trilogy, especially with all of the changes that happened in Gormenghast. I'll definitely be finishing this series soon.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7325030476582832937" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_373893061" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/373893061" />
    <a title="Kim" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11161028-kim"><img alt="Kim" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1562512310p2/11161028.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/373893061?book_show_action=true">Oct 21, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kim" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kim" href="/user/show/11161028-kim">Kim</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/11282431-gormenghast">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11161028-kim?shelf=owned-books">owned-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11161028-kim?shelf=big-read-100-read">big-read-100-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11161028-kim?shelf=oz-oct-2020">oz-oct-2020</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer373893061&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer373893061" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15449675610479104155">A wonderful sequel to Titus Groan as I was soon engrossed once more in life at Gormenghast, five years on from the end of the first book, being re-introduced to familiar characters from the first book and many new ones, notably Professor Bellgrove, the headmaster, and his team of teachers with another great set of names like Fluke, Perch-Prism, Cutflower, Shred, Spiregrain, Flannelcat and Deadyawn. It suddenly dawned on me as the death toll mounted during book 2, how many characters were actuall</span>
  <span id="freeText15449675610479104155" style="display:none">A wonderful sequel to Titus Groan as I was soon engrossed once more in life at Gormenghast, five years on from the end of the first book, being re-introduced to familiar characters from the first book and many new ones, notably Professor Bellgrove, the headmaster, and his team of teachers with another great set of names like Fluke, Perch-Prism, Cutflower, Shred, Spiregrain, Flannelcat and Deadyawn. It suddenly dawned on me as the death toll mounted during book 2, how many characters were actually killed off in the first book too! <br />The main themes of the book are Steerpike's continuing efforts to further his own agenda and strengthen his position; Titus's rebellion against the restrictive traditions governing his every action; Irma Prunesquallor's search for a husband and the effects on the castle of a monumental flood. The writing as ever was thoroughly atmospheric and absorbing and I just felt as if I was there again. Really looking forward to the third in the trilogy - 9.5/10.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15449675610479104155" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">
          

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review373893061">
  <td colspan="2">
    <div class="updateActionLinks">
        <a class="right flag" rel="nofollow" id="flag_link373893061" title="Flag this review as inappropriate." href="/flagged/new?resource_id=373893061&amp;resource_type=Review&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F258392.Gormenghast">flag</a>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2038365363" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2038365363" />
    <a title="Aiden Heavilin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/65067206-aiden-heavilin"><img alt="Aiden Heavilin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1515122208p2/65067206.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2038365363?book_show_action=true">Jun 23, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Aiden Heavilin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Aiden Heavilin" href="/user/show/65067206-aiden-heavilin">Aiden Heavilin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2038365363" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3043244975888536287">To write a review of "Gormenghast" is as futile an endeavor as writing a review of a game of chess between two masters. For, in the end, that is what the book comes down to, a contest between one player, wise and battle-hardened, and another, youthful yet fierce. The game is between the old and the new. Peake coordinated the match, provided the pieces (his characters, each intricately detailed with unique functions), and provided the board (his castle, 'Gormenghast', that stony monster). This bo</span>
  <span id="freeText3043244975888536287" style="display:none">To write a review of "Gormenghast" is as futile an endeavor as writing a review of a game of chess between two masters. For, in the end, that is what the book comes down to, a contest between one player, wise and battle-hardened, and another, youthful yet fierce. The game is between the old and the new. Peake coordinated the match, provided the pieces (his characters, each intricately detailed with unique functions), and provided the board (his castle, 'Gormenghast', that stony monster). This book is nothing more than Peake's mad attempts to record what he saw of this chess game between the two-ideals, this fierce battle fought with the figures of his imagination.<br /><br />If the first book, "Titus Groan" was the opening, "Gormenghast" recounts the middlegame and finale of this match. Titus and Steerpike are the king and queen respectively of the new's brigade, one of them unsure, tentative, able to traverse the board only in little hops, and the other bold, fierce, streaking on diagonals and columns and rows, taking pieces at will. The dual bishops of the old's army, Cora and Clarice, are taken immediately, and a hard-fought battle manages to eliminate the old's tenacious rook Barquentine. But the king of the old is a stubborn fellow, he is ritual himself, the oppressive crush and squeeze of the past full-force, it will take something beyond the board to destroy him.<br /><br />------------------------------------------------<br /><br />The fact that it is eventually a Storm that destroys Gormenghast, not some explosion detonated by Steerpike, or some rebellion amongst the carvers, indicates the futility of Peake's characters. They can do little to change their surroundings, when they rebel, it is against the bulwarks of something larger than any of them. In the end, whether or not Steerpike had remained Swelter's kitchen boy, whether or not Titus had met The Thing, whether or not Sepulchrave had been devoured by the owls in the Tower of Flints, things would've turned out the same. The storm that floods Gormenghast and renders worthless its ritual and its tradition emenates from the hand of no mortal. It is but Chance himself, that mischievous wretch, approaching the hard fought chess game between the masters and upending the board with snickering glee. The pieces scatter across the ground. Those monstrous squares that held them prisoner have been vanquished. Only now might they live free.<br /><br /><br />------------------------------------------------<br /><br />This is an awesome book, and I'll use the word awesome even though it's been so devalued; it's the only word that might do it justice. Not only does Peake arrange this brilliant chess game, he records it with humor, with an artist's eye, with dark, mellifluous prose that seeks the painting in the battle and the song in the screams of torment. Not only does he fashion beautiful pieces to populate his board, he cares about each one, he has infused each one with madness and beauty. Not only has he wrought a board, Gormenghast itself, from the bones of the earth, he has rendered it a thing of both terror and wonder, of dreams and of nightmares married together.<br /><br />And yet, having read it, I feel I have only begun to plumb its mysteries, I feel that it will take many more readings of 'Titus Groan' and 'Gormenghast' before I understand the subtleties of Peake's imagination. <br /><br />Honestly, I can't wait.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3043244975888536287" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_38651203" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/38651203" />
    <a title="The Literary Chick" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1751099-the-literary-chick"><img alt="The Literary Chick" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1532543079p2/1751099.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/38651203?book_show_action=true">Nov 25, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="The Literary Chick" class="user" itemprop="url" name="The Literary Chick" href="/user/show/1751099-the-literary-chick">The Literary Chick</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1751099-the-literary-chick?shelf=gothic">gothic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1751099-the-literary-chick?shelf=literature">literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1751099-the-literary-chick?shelf=dark">dark</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer38651203" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5066920763033646326">The Gormenghast Trilogy is amazing. I don't know whether it's because it was written by an artist, but it is without a doubt the most painterly novel I've ever read. Peake's use of language incredibly beautiful and visual. Steerpike becomes so malignantly evil in the book, at some points I could only read short bits at a time. And the operative word is "becomes". Peake draws Steerpike not merely as a one dimensional character, but allows you to see his mental and physical disintegration over tim</span>
  <span id="freeText5066920763033646326" style="display:none">The Gormenghast Trilogy is amazing. I don't know whether it's because it was written by an artist, but it is without a doubt the most painterly novel I've ever read. Peake's use of language incredibly beautiful and visual. Steerpike becomes so malignantly evil in the book, at some points I could only read short bits at a time. And the operative word is "becomes". Peake draws Steerpike not merely as a one dimensional character, but allows you to see his mental and physical disintegration over time. In the movie you're led to believe that he does have some feeling for Fushia. Not in the book. He is the most calculating, Machiavellian character ever created. The movie was a very good recreation, but not entirely faithful to the book. I found myself so upset about what happens to Fushia, and so annoyed at Titus for, well, I don't want to give it away, you'd think these people existed. I sloughed my way through Titus Alone, base upon the strength of the prior novels, Titus Groan and Gormenghast. Peake still writes beautifully, but you almost have to say he does so in a vacuum, because the novel is so-so, to say the least. Titus can be such an annoying character and it's just not as tight plot-wise as the other two. There are good parts, but I found myself guiltily skimming for them, which is something I wouldn't dream of doing in the other two.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5066920763033646326" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2093430044" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11873442820348069301">The stage is built, my blueprint (which I often draw of literary villages/castles, etc., having been inspired to do so by Mark Bennett and his fabulous/fantasy work entitled "TV Sets: Fantasy Blueprints of Classic TV Homes"- yes, exactly, you might think, that's where Ginger and Mary Anne must have sun bathed!) is drawn, I'm ready for another visit to Gormenghast. From a literary point of view, "Titus Groan" is complete, in and of itself: that's why we really can't call Gormenghast part of a tri</span>
  <span id="freeText11873442820348069301" style="display:none">The stage is built, my blueprint (which I often draw of literary villages/castles, etc., having been inspired to do so by Mark Bennett and his fabulous/fantasy work entitled "TV Sets: Fantasy Blueprints of Classic TV Homes"- yes, exactly, you might think, that's where Ginger and Mary Anne must have sun bathed!) is drawn, I'm ready for another visit to Gormenghast. From a literary point of view, "Titus Groan" is complete, in and of itself: that's why we really can't call Gormenghast part of a trilogy, as it too stands alone. But the 77th Earl of Gormenghast is a rebel, and when the final showdown for power commences... oh, just read it, as you MUST if you appreciate sensational, beautifully written and imagined, literature and feel like you haven't found anything truly awesome this year. Or this decade. Or even this century.</span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('jLBkMgK2kFBN+TYP8ec1lLFENZ5Xxvesr92YyzHrB6m2lg3guehZOOrSgm4GEj9LZxvSVplLecv0o956/Kj1Mw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('qLLM48I6bzzFOiDrB/vtAMXcU/ZyDaPViYS2S97I8pGSlKUxeWSmVGIRlIrwDuffE4O0PryALbLS+vD6E4sACw==')})
  }

Lovelace, however, proves himself to be an untrustworthy rake whose vague promises of marriage are accompanied by unwelcome and increasingly brutal sexual advances. And yet, Clarissa finds his charm alluring, her scrupulous sense of virtue tinged with unconfessed desire. \n\nTold through a complex series of interweaving letters, &quot;Clarissa&quot; is a richly ambiguous study of a fatally attracted couple and a work of astonishing power and immediacy. A huge success when it first appeared in 1747, and translated into French and German, it remains one of the greatest of all European novels. Its rich ambiguities - our sense of Clarissa\'s scrupulous virtue tinged with intimations of her capacity for self-deception in matters of sex; the wicked and amusing faces of Lovelace, who must be       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/22018.Mervyn_Peake">About Mervyn Peake</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/22018.Mervyn_Peake"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1341040504p3/22018.jpg);'></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/22018.Mervyn_Peake">Mervyn Peake

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer11874917513780799807">Mervyn Laurence Peake was an English modernist writer, artist, poet and illustrator. He is best known for what are usually referred to as the <i>Gormenghast books</i>, though the <i>Titus</i> books would be more accurate: the three works that exist were the beginning of what Peake conceived as a lengthy cycle, following his protagonist Titus Groan from cradle to grave, but Peake's untimely death prevented compl</span>
  <span id="freeText11874917513780799807" style="display:none">Mervyn Laurence Peake was an English modernist writer, artist, poet and illustrator. He is best known for what are usually referred to as the <i>Gormenghast books</i>, though the <i>Titus</i> books would be more accurate: the three works that exist were the beginning of what Peake conceived as a lengthy cycle, following his protagonist Titus Groan from cradle to grave, but Peake's untimely death prevented completion of the cycle, which is now commonly but erroneously referred to as a trilogy. They are sometimes compared to the work of his older contemporary J.R.R. Tolkien, but his surreal fiction was influenced by his early love for Charles Dickens and Robert Louis Stevenson rather than Tolkien's studies of mythology and philology.<br /><br />Peake also wrote poetry and literary nonsense in verse form, short stories for adults and children ("Letters from a Lost Uncle"), stage and radio plays, and <i>Mr Pye</i>, a relatively tightly-structured novel in which God implicitly mocks the evangelical pretensions and cosy world-view of the eponymous hero.<br /><br />Peake first made his reputation as a painter and illustrator during the 1930s and 1940s, when he lived in London, and he was commissioned to produce portraits of well-known people. A collection of these drawings is still in the possession of his family. Although he gained little popular success in his lifetime, his work was highly respected by his peers, and his friends included Dylan Thomas and Graham Greene. His works are now included in the collections of the National Portrait Gallery and the Imperial War Museum.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11874917513780799807" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
